Uncoiling Season 
Van Itallie's "The erpent," which ex-

plores th e Book of G nesis and relates it 
to modern experience through th e 
Kennedy assassination, is schedu led as 
the first production of th e season at the 
U SD Theatre. 

First performed in May 1968 in Rome 
by the experimental Open Theater, the 
play now has arned recognition as a 
milestone in contemporary drama. 
U SD's performance of the work will be 
its premiere in an Diego. 

Six performances will be given, each at 
8 p.m ., Nov. 10, 11 , 12, 16, 17, and 18. 
Admission is $2.50 for general admission 
and $1 .25 for students. ubscription rates 
for the four-play season are also available 
to the general public at a cost of $7.50 - a 
savi ng of $2 .50. Students may receive a 
subscription rate of $3.75. Tickets are 
available at the UCSD Drama Depart-
ment, located at the Theatre, MC 203, or 
by telephone at 453-2000, extension 2491. 

" There are no individual characters 
that run throughout the play, but many 
characterizations," said Dr . Arthur 
Wagner, director of "The Serpent" and 
new chairman of UC D's Drama Depart-
ment. One of the rea sons for selecting 
the play, W agner sa id, was its ability to 
involve many studen ts in the production , 
10 women and 7 men. While the play 
uses minima l scenery and props, the 
many soun d effects will be produced by 

th actors themse lves . A few primitive 
percussion instruments, researched and 
recreated by th e students, will also hI' 
used . 

"'The erpent' is not a pl~y in the 
co nventional sense," sai d Wagner. " It is 
a real group creation and a grea t d al of 
improvisation is necessary. However, th(' 
play also demands extreme precision and 
a great deal of physical work and eHort. " 

It should be looked upon more a~ a 
"c('fl'mony" than a "p lay" said Wagnl'r 
The audience is symbolically a congrega-
tion with the a tor moving freely amon g 
the audience and never moving froln 
view. 

"And yet, i~ a sense, both the actors 
and the audience are the celeb rant s of 
th ceremony," remarked Wagnel 

·" Wh il there is an end to the ceremon y, 
thc ref lection stimulated by the plav 
should continue after the ce remony has 
ended. 

" The play is not so much about 
religion," said Wagner, "but about our 
image of God . The play uggests 'So 
man created God. What for? To se t limits 
on himself. '" 

Another theme is the play Wagner said 
was th relationship of a killer to his 
victim, mirrored in the scene of Cain and 
Abel and in the scene of Kennedy's 
assassi n at ion. 

"The fascinating tlJjng 
abou t the Cain and Abel 
incident ," observed 
Wagner, "i that while it 
occured to Cain to kill 

' Abel, it did not occur to 
him that killing Abel would 
mean his death , for it was 
the first murder in histor y.' 

The play is also about 
innocence. " By acceptin g 
life, one must give up in-
nocence," said Wagnrr 
" When Eve eats of the trr !' 
of life, she i taking on lif' 
Before thai time, she was 
not able to se anv 
differentiation betwe n 
herself and the Olh<'r 
animals in the ga rden.

1. Honorable Day 
From Battery Park. only the hazy profile of the Statue 

of Liberty is directly discernible. Sometimes, when the 
ha71' momentarily dissipates, it does not matter; only 
th(> back is visible. 

Two men stood on the shore at Battery Park. throw-
ing hits of colored glass into the bay.b!C'low. As they 
co ncentrated upon their tardy effort. the granite mass 
loomed in Ihe distan e before their eyes .. -

" Have you ever been there?" said one of th men. 
fina lly noticing th(> statue in the distance. 

"Only once. When I was a kid." answered the other . 
"Did you climb 10 the top?" 
"Nah. I cou ldn ' t make it. Very f w people make it to

Reply to 
Stunley 
Chodorow 
Ed itN: 

R(ading Mr. Chodorow' 
lette; (T.T. 10/ 31172) , one 
might almo t conclude he 
exhibits the jealou y of a 
far-removed program 
director for an active in-
terested teaching assistant. 
Blaming Jeff's attitude on 
" personal hang-ups" does 
not confront the issue-it 
merely gives Chodorow a 
chance to show off his acid 
backbiting techniques . 

Mr. Chodorow claims 
real enjoyment comes from 
a deep understanding of 
the great books (8 in eight 
weeks) presented in the 
course. This is probably 
true for those few persons 
who have voluntarily spe-
cialized in Western liter-
ature. 

However, I believe the 
Chairman of the 
Humanities equence is · 
missing the real point of his 
entire program. The 
program exists to insure 
that Revelle College's 
mainly scie nce-oriented 

students gain knowledge 
and facility in the broad 
area of the humanities . This 
is an excellent purposf'; we 
all agree on the hazards of 
too ndfrow a specia I iza-
tion . 

Yet the program directors 
have built their sequence 
upon two false assump-
tions: 1) that Revelle 
students would not under-
take to study anything in 
the area of humanities on 
their own (and therefore 
must have it rammed down 
their throats), and 2) that 
"Huma nities" consists sole-
ly of the study of Western 
Civilization, and any non-
Western studies have no 
relevance to our culture 
whatsoever . 

With regard to the first 
false promise, let me say 
that I do not intend to miss 
the chance of studying as 
many interesting non-
science subjects as I can at 
this institution-and I be-
lieve that there are many 
tudents like me. I simply 

must be inspired by enjoy-
ment, not force , and that 
therefore we should be 
allowed some choice of I 
courses or subjects within 

the existing sequence . 
Personally , after fifteen 

yea rs of American institu-
tions, I have had my fill of 
Western studies. My own 
choice would be to study 
ancient and mod rn 
African Civiliza-
tion-something I can real-
ly get turned on about. 

urely the majority of 
students have some field of 
particular interest, whe-
ther in Western or non-
Western civilizations. 

It is a crime to maintain 
that our culture is not in-
fluenced by African, Asian, 
or Eastern ones. Certainly 
we can gain a far better 
view of our own culture by 
studying the juxtaposition 
of ours with others. And 
would not the aim of the 
program be better served 
by students' enthusiastic in-
quiry than by forced plod-
ding? 

Steven Rooke 
a srudent (remember us?)

United Crusade

Rotten Clear Through 
Fred Driscoll 

Those who watched the CBS Evening 
ews last Tue day need not read thi s. 

Walter Cronkite devoted 10 minutes 10 
the second part of hi news team's in-
vE'stigation of the Watergate E'spionage 
attempt by th e Nixon Administration . 
The report detailed the transactions to 
Jnd from a ecret milliondollar intelli-
gence and operations fund which was 
kept in ca h In a safe in the offices of the 
Committee to Re-elect the President. 

The fund was controlled by persons as 
high as Attorney General Mitchell and 
Executive Chief of Staff Haldemann . The 
money was used for bugging and es-
pionage uch as at the Watergate offices 
of the Democratic Party ; for spying on 
the lives of Democrati candidates; and 
for provocateur operations (forced 
letters, impersonating phone calls, etc.) 
to cripplE' the Democratic party . 
Cronkite's conclusion was that we were 
witnessing an exceedingly serious threat 
to the political future of America. 

There are many p rsons who will 
never hear these facts . Others will se 
them as mere political mud-slinging. 
Most people will believe the charge 
have merit, but they have become im-
mune to caring about wrong-
doing-"everybody does it, why, ju t 
look at the Vietnam mess ." Indeed, just 
look at America . 

When the Watergat case first hit the 

new, it seemed almost funny. Five per-
sons were arrE'sted in the middle of thr 
night in Democratic National Head-
quarters with cameras, burglar'S tool ~, 
and bugging equipment. One of th E'm, 
Jam s McCord, Jr ., was then serving a~ 
security chief for the Committee to Rr-
elect the Pr iden!. ecret Campaign 
contributions of $114,000 were tracE'd to 
the bank account of Bernard Barker, also 
arre ted at Watergate. 

The funny part came in the Adminis-
tration response . Within the govern-
ment and in corporations implicated 
monetarily, the word was passed that all 
statements had to be ok'd at the top." 0 
comment" became the comment of the 
day. Then conservative columnists such 
as James Kilpatrick spread counter-
rumors: the Democrats had made a 
ecret deal with Castro for large sums of 

money, and Barker and McCord werE' 
patriots trying to uncover the deal. 
Agnew put forth the theory that the 
whole thing was "set up" by "some-
body" who wanted to "embaras the 
Republicans./I Pennzoil President 
William Liedtke (who supplied a suit-
case of $700,000 of which $89,000 in 
Mexican checks went to Barker) wa 110 
longer to be found , and the company 
wouldn't supply a picture of biography 
of their own president. Very funny .

INTRODUCTION 
How was your (fill .in) class last winter quarter? Chances 

a student distributed a pilot course and Professor 
Evoluation form at the end of it, and your response to that 
questionnaire is contained inside. 

Not all of the classes offered last winter are represented 
here only because there were nat enough students on the 

luation committee to caver them all. Although incamplete, 
believe that all information collected should be made 
ila ble to the university community. We have printed these 

now so they can be of use to students registering for 
winter quarter . 

HOW IT CAME TO BE 
an encompassing compus-wide evaluation 

rogram has been around for a long time. But, unlike most 
college campuses, it had not token hold until last year 
the Students' Educational Change and Development 

organized a group of students into the Ad Hoc 
uotion Committee. Those concerned students conducted a 

study of various evaluatian methods and finally settled on the 
sixteen'point questionnaire which was used here lost year. 
Working as closely as possible with faculty , the students 
monaged to cover a large number of the classes during the 
final week of the quarter. The questionnaires were ad· 
m istered during the lost five minutes of those classes and 
COllected Immediately afterwards . It was the students' inten· 

tion to computerize the results, but due to production work 
load this has not happened until now. 

This publication, then, shows the actual response figures 
from the evaluation of lost winter, combined with descriptions 
written from the camments (Question 16). Instructions on how 
to read the results are given belaw .

WHY A STUDENTS' COURSE AND 

PROFESSOR EVALUATION 

In addition , thiS evaluation IS the beginning of on effort to 
establish a comprehensive program for the study and 
Improvement of the educational environmen of UCSD, 
involving a cooperative effort between student and faculty.

UCSD STUDENT EVALUATION OF COURSES 

The evaluations will be public information and will be made available to anyone 
interested. This evaluation is run entirely by the students of UCSD 

Your answers to this questionnaire will provide information to fellow students which will 
help them decide whether or not to toke th is course. In addition, they will provide 
feedback to the instructor about his own teaching ability and to the department and 

C~oncellor about how students rate his teaching ability. 

GENERAL QUESTIONS (high rating) 7 6 5 4 3 2 1 (low rating)

2 Ro te the value of the course as a whole. 
3. How much was your appreciation for the subject material increased? 
4 How strongly would you recommend this course to other students?

5. How well does the professor keep your attention? 
6. Rate the instructor's speaking ability . 
7 How organized were the lectures? 
8. How much did the prafessor encourage student participation in class? 
9 Rate the instructor's enthusiasm and interest in his area .

PHYSICAL EDUCATION 

You may have noticed that there are no responses shown 
for P.E. closses. The P.E. depo rtment was by for the most 
receptive to the evaluation program, even to the point that 
they conducted the evaluation on their own using the 
standard student form . The results of those questionnaires 
were all extremely good and only served to further mirror the 
need for and interest in physical education courses at this 
school. We would like to thank the P.E. deportment for their 
support of our program and for offering 10 work so closely 
wilh students in th is effort.
